  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_4c813d05-02c8-4364-a278-25cb60447086'
      });
    }

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer15819754735228333606">A world classic. "The Golden Bough" describes our ancestors' primitive methods of worship, sex practices, strange rituals and festivals. Disproving the popular thought that primitive life was simple, this monumental survey shows that savage man was enmeshed in a tangle of magic, taboos, and superstitions. Revealed here is the evolution of man from savagery to civilization,</span>
  <span id="freeText15819754735228333606" style="display:none">A world classic. "The Golden Bough" describes our ancestors' primitive methods of worship, sex practices, strange rituals and festivals. Disproving the popular thought that primitive life was simple, this monumental survey shows that savage man was enmeshed in a tangle of magic, taboos, and superstitions. Revealed here is the evolution of man from savagery to civilization, from the modification of his weird and often bloodthirsty customs to the entry of lasting moral, ethical, and spiritual values.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15819754735228333606" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/656073-argh-i-just-realised-that-the-golden">Argh! I just realised that the Golden Bough edition I bought when I was 19 is not the complete edition (yeah, I kinda buy books for later, you see&quot;) and the other volumes are pretty much impossible to find in the same edition. What would be the best digital edition of the thing?</a>
</div>

<ul class='questionFooterActions'>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/5361346-susan"><img alt="" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_100x100-6433b08cbe793211f55e184d39e84c4d.png" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/5361346-susan">Susan</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">If you still haven't read it yet, just read a good abridged version. I'm pretty sure the Oxford abridgement contains all the relevant hypotheses. Fraz<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">If you still haven't read it yet, just read a good abridged version. I'm pretty sure the Oxford abridgement contains all the relevant hypotheses. Frazer's various original editions will contain many more examples to support his various hypotheses and will make for extremely gruelling reading. The Oxford abridgement is wonderful stuff, but each argument is broken down into lots of smaller arguments, which are in turn supported by up to about a couple dozen examples - fatiguing to read. The unabridged editions will simply hammer the points home, or possibly be more of a compendium to dip into rather than something to read straight through.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_179244149" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/179244149" />
    <a title="Jan-Maat" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5617661-jan-maat"><img alt="Jan-Maat" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1407579968p2/5617661.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/179244149?book_show_action=true">Jun 24, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jan-Maat" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jan-Maat" href="/user/show/5617661-jan-maat">Jan-Maat</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title=" " href="/book/show/6272285-the-golden-bough">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=religion">religion</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=19th-century">19th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=myths">myths</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer179244149" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5955849433557302985">I read an abridged version of this some years ago that I picked up in a bookshop for a pound - the output of a cheap publisher. It was a slow and awkward read, possibly because of the abridgement, but the original was long and appeared in numerous editions each of which tended to get more elaborate during Frazer's lifetime.<br/><br/>The opening echoes Gibbon's <i>Decline and Fall of the Roman Empire</i>, the British scholar in Italy looks over the landscape and allows a vision of the past, the product of their c</span>
  <span id="freeText5955849433557302985" style="display:none">I read an abridged version of this some years ago that I picked up in a bookshop for a pound - the output of a cheap publisher. It was a slow and awkward read, possibly because of the abridgement, but the original was long and appeared in numerous editions each of which tended to get more elaborate during Frazer's lifetime.<br /><br />The opening echoes Gibbon's <i>Decline and Fall of the Roman Empire</i>, the British scholar in Italy looks over the landscape and allows a vision of the past, the product of their classical learning, to sweep over them. In Frazer's case though this was not a vision of the City of Rome but of the myth of the King of the Grove at Nemi. The practise at Nemi was that there was a priest of the Goddess Diana who became priest by killing the current occupant of the office in single combat, and who would then be the priest until they in turn were killed by a younger, stronger applicant for the role. The priest was generally, maybe eventually always, an escaped slave, at least by the time that that the Classical writers were mentioning the practise - a few years, however brief, as Priest of Diana were better than a long life of slavery.<br /><br />Frazer felt that the central idea of the cycle of eternal renewal was the foundational idea of religious and magical thinking manifesting itself from the most 'savage' culture to Christianity. The Christian connection was soft pedalled since one couldn't print that kind of thing in Victorian Britain, but by implication, Christ's death and resurrection was simply in his view just one more repetition of the death and rebirth of the natural world, the symbolic or actual death of a ritual figure magically required to ensure the rebirth of seed crops every year.<br /><br />This Frazer set out to demonstrate by stock piling examples of this kind of myth from rural European corn kings and nineteenth century harvest songs to the cult celebration of the death of Adonis as well as everything in between. In this way it still functions as a convenient treasure trove of myths, stories and beliefs irrespective of the validity of his thesis. Having said that the choice and arrangement of his material is determined by his goals.<br /><br />What I found most interesting was the snippets from the disappearing culture of the Victorian British countryside. Machines are all well and good, but they don't sing songs or trade their savings to buy the horseman's word off a stranger in an ale house <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ the horseman's word was a word, command or maybe a spell, that if you knew it would allow you absolute magical control over horses - a useful thing to know in a horse-powered countryside <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ anyhow handing over a few guineas for the horseman's word makes a change from selling your wife to a sailor for a few mugs of booze at a summer fair in the style of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/56759.The_Mayor_of_Casterbridge" title="The Mayor of Casterbridge by Thomas Hardy" rel="nofollow noopener">The Mayor of Casterbridge</a> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br /> <br />It seems all a little <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17841.Foucault_s_Pendulum" title="Foucault's Pendulum by Umberto Eco" rel="nofollow noopener">Foucault's Pendulum</a> on the one hand, in its assumption of a meta-narrative and its urgent quest to reveal it, while on the other it speaks to the centrality of Christianity in Frazer's mind in that even in his effort to put it in an explanatory framework he is clearly still engaged with the central idea of the resurrection. Rather than get away from it he has expanded it and imposed it (or discovered it depending on your point of view) on everything else. In that respect in my imagination Frazer is something like the archetypal angry atheist, still so firmly possessed by their religious background that they carry it with them were ever they go.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5955849433557302985" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer13248158441451427392">The Golden Bough: A Study in Comparative Religion = The Golden Bough: A Study in Religion and Magic, James George Frazer<br/><br/>The Golden Bough was first published in two volumes in 1890; in three volumes in 1900; and in twelve volumes in the third edition, published 1906–15. It has also been published in several different one-volume abridgments. <br/><br/>The work was aimed at a wide literate audience raised on tales as told in such publications as Thomas Bulfinch's The Age of Fable, or Stories of Gods and Hero</span>
  <span id="freeText13248158441451427392" style="display:none">The Golden Bough: A Study in Comparative Religion = The Golden Bough: A Study in Religion and Magic, James George Frazer<br /><br />The Golden Bough was first published in two volumes in 1890; in three volumes in 1900; and in twelve volumes in the third edition, published 1906–15. It has also been published in several different one-volume abridgments. <br /><br />The work was aimed at a wide literate audience raised on tales as told in such publications as Thomas Bulfinch's The Age of Fable, or Stories of Gods and Heroes (1855). The influence of The Golden Bough on contemporary European literature and thought was substantial.<br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: روز دوازدهم ماه ژوئن سال 2005 میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: شاخه زرین: پژوهشی در جادو و دین؛ نویسنده: جیمز جورج فریزر؛ مترجم: کاظم فیروزمند، تهران، آگاه، چاپ نخست 1383، چاپ دوم 1384، در 861ص؛ شابک 9644162196؛ چاپ سوم و چهارم 1386؛ چاپ پنجم 1387، چاپ ششم 1388؛ چاپ هفتم 1392؛ چاپ هشتم 1394، چاپ نهم و دهم 1396، چاپ یازدهم 1397؛ <br /><br />عنوان: شاخه زرین؛ نویسنده: جیمز جورج فریزر؛ مترجم: مسعود خیرخواه، تهران، نشر علم، 1388، در 1100ص؛ شابک 9789642240142؛ موضوع: اسطوره شناسی - از نویسندگان بریتانیایی - سده 19م<br /><br />پژوهش تطبیقی و همسنجی «شاخهٔ زرین»، عنوان یکی از برجسته‌ ترین آثار «جیمز فریزر» است، که دارای شهرت جهانگیرست؛ پژوهشی با رویکردی اجتماعی، با تکیه بر دو محور: «اسطوره» و «دین» است؛<br /><br />کتاب به پژوهش در مبانی «خرافه‌ پرستی»، «ادیان ابتدایی»، «باورها»، و «مناسک مردمان اولیه» می‌پردازد، و بر آنست تا این ادعا را ثابت کند، که سیر اندیشه ی انسان‌ها، برای توجیه رخدادهای زندگی، و طبیعت پیرامون‌شان، و نیز برای گره‌گشایی، یا گریز از پیچیدگی‌های زندگی (حیات) ست، و اینکه چگونه نخست به جادو، و خرافه‌ ها روی آوردند، و پس از آن به دین گرویدند، و سرانجام از دین به علم متمایل گشتند<br /><br />ایشان در این کتاب، به دنبال یافتن وجه مشترک: «ادیان»، و اساطیر مردمان جهان بوده اند؛ عنوان «شاخه ی زرین»، برگرفته از تابلویی، با همین نام، از «ویلیام ترنر (1775میلادی - 1851میلادی)» نقاش نامدار بریتانیایی است، و در واقع اشاره به درختی مقدس دارد، که شکننده ی شاخه‌ ای از آن، می‌توانست برای تصاحب منصب «کهانتِ معبد دایانا» در ایتالیا، با کاهن پیشین معبد، مبارزه کند؛ <br />کتاب «شاخه ی زرین» در نخستین چاپ، در سال 1890میلادی، در دو جلد منتشر شد؛ در ویرایش دوم در سال 1900میلادی، به سه جلد رسید، و در ویرایش سوم که به شکلی تفصیلی بین سال‌های 1906میلادی تا سال 1915میلادی، به‌ وسیله ی نگارنده انجام شد، با افزودن شواهد و نمونه‌ های بسیار، شمار مجلدات کتاب به دوازده جلد رسید؛<br /><br />نویسنده ی کتاب «جیمز فریزر»، در سال‌های پایانی عمر خویش نیز، پیوستی به کتاب افزودند، که شمار مجلدات کتاب را، به عدد سیزده مجلد رساند؛ در سال 1990میلادی، خلاصه‌ ای از کتاب «شاخه ی زرین»، با مقدمه، ویرایش، و گزینش دقیق «رابرت فریزر (نوه ی نویسنده)»، در یک جلد منتشر شد؛ همین کتاب یکجلدی را، جناب «کاظم فیروزمند»، به واژه های پارسایی ترجمه کردند، تا پس از گذشت یکصد و چهارده سال از نخستین چاپ اثر، نخستین برگردان «شاخه ی زرین» به زبان پارسایی باشد؛ «شاخهٔ زرین»، اثری ارزشمند، و پژوهشی ست، که ستایش‌ها و نکوهش‌های بسیاری را در پی داشته، اما در کل، سودبخشی ژرف این کتاب بر نویسندگان و پژوهشگران پس از ایشان در این حوزه را هزگزی نمی‌توان نادیده انگاشت<br /><br />تاریخ بهنگام رسانی 19/03/199هجری خورشید؛ ا. شربیانی</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer4831300107644134210">Influential without bound and ere-breaking of ground, this is undeniably a major modern classic that reshaped its entire field. Of course, most of Frazier's theses have been broadly discredited, but it's not like you're studying comparative mythology to build bridges with it (although it's been proposed that unsold copies of Joseph Campbell, shredded to a fine mist, would provide high-quality industrial weathering and cheap insulation suitable for the Third World).<br/><br/>That having been said, this boo</span>
  <span id="freeText4831300107644134210" style="display:none">Influential without bound and ere-breaking of ground, this is undeniably a major modern classic that reshaped its entire field. Of course, most of Frazier's theses have been broadly discredited, but it's not like you're studying comparative mythology to build bridges with it (although it's been proposed that unsold copies of Joseph Campbell, shredded to a fine mist, would provide high-quality industrial weathering and cheap insulation suitable for the Third World).<br /><br />That having been said, this book is incredibly fucking long. Longer than you think, and then twice as long again. It was truly an ordeal to complete -- if you've not the copious free time of an American high school student, you're probably better off scanning Harry Potter for the hidden Golden Dawn / fertility rite references and mumbling lies when this book comes up at parties.<br /><br />Practice with papa dank:<br /><br />"mumble mumble mumble mumble Year King"<br />"mumble mumble mumble mumble Religious Evolution"<br />"mumble mumble mumble I'm going to open a stinking apostrophe factory with Robert Burns whenever I get back to bonnie Scotland"</span>

<div id="review_12887422" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/12887422" />
    <a title="Michael J.J. Tiffany" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/727763-michael-j-j-tiffany"><img alt="Michael J.J. Tiffany" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1199848138p2/727763.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/12887422?book_show_action=true">Jan 18, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael J.J. Tiffany" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael J.J. Tiffany" href="/user/show/727763-michael-j-j-tiffany">Michael J.J. Tiffany</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140189315" href="/book/show/1431936.The_Golden_Bough">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer12887422" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17628769778061579518">Discovering The Golden Bough, and then Graves' The White Goddess (which owes a critically huge debt to the Golden Bough), was a life-changing time for me that recast the stories I had vacuumed up at that age, from Greek myths to Kipling, as about something more than their contents or even the authors intent. It was first published over 100 years ago; still, nothing can get a boy into that modernist, meta- meta- meta- perspective on society like <i>The Golden Bough</i>. Of course it's only fair that we </span>
  <span id="freeText17628769778061579518" style="display:none">Discovering The Golden Bough, and then Graves' The White Goddess (which owes a critically huge debt to the Golden Bough), was a life-changing time for me that recast the stories I had vacuumed up at that age, from Greek myths to Kipling, as about something more than their contents or even the authors intent. It was first published over 100 years ago; still, nothing can get a boy into that modernist, meta- meta- meta- perspective on society like <i>The Golden Bough</i>. Of course it's only fair that we turn the lens on Frazer and see much of the value of the book now as arising from what it can tell us about its own time and place. In that sense it's worth reading just to understand Wittgenstein's references to it. But man, when I was a kid, this book was like a drug. All of humanity, it seemed to say, even the irrational parts -- especially the irrational parts -- was understandable (and improvable!), if you just studied it properly. Ah, Victorian optimism.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17628769778061579518" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_900763813" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/900763813" />
    <a title="E. G." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5895486-e-g"><img alt="E. G." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1541991932p2/5895486.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

<div id="review_158255640" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/158255640" />
    <a title="Cassandra Kay Silva" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2309246-cassandra-kay-silva"><img alt="Cassandra Kay Silva" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1595054016p2/2309246.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/158255640?book_show_action=true">Apr 01, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cassandra Kay Silva" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cassandra Kay Silva" href="/user/show/2309246-cassandra-kay-silva">Cassandra Kay Silva</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781605209364" href="/book/show/7396810-the-golden-bough">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2309246-cassandra-kay-silva?shelf=religion">religion</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2309246-cassandra-kay-silva?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer158255640" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3827861902038864936">This is such an important work. If you take it from the perspective of what it is, an anthology of rituals and belief systems found in religious and non religious cultures across the globe. As some other readers have pointed out it is not linear, it is also not well coordinated in way of connecting points and making/laying out statements about those points. But what it is absolutely superb and unbeatable in, is its exhaustive amount of information. I did read the full version, and the sheer amou</span>
  <span id="freeText3827861902038864936" style="display:none">This is such an important work. If you take it from the perspective of what it is, an anthology of rituals and belief systems found in religious and non religious cultures across the globe. As some other readers have pointed out it is not linear, it is also not well coordinated in way of connecting points and making/laying out statements about those points. But what it is absolutely superb and unbeatable in, is its exhaustive amount of information. I did read the full version, and the sheer amount of stories and references and ideas that are brought about in this book is so mind blowing. The thing that makes it so worth reading is just how different this information is from what is generally given in this category. He really just hammers out example after example from so many different cultures with so many different ideas that you can't help but make connections in your own mind even if the author does not do it explicitly for you. Its fascinating information, and better yet it stories that you absolutely don't find elsewhere in this sort of study, and that is rare. The stories about women even during start of menstruation are completely un expected and yet so vastly interesting. Women being shut up in cages without even sunlight, women being hidden away in huts and buried under the sand. Its so bizarre and yet so well referenced that you can help but be interested. Usually with books on myths and magic they circumnavigate the same popular tales and same popular belief systems. Frazer takes a bit of a different approach. He looks at older cultures, he looks at cultures like the aboriginal tribes in Australia and island/secluded sectors for his research. He lets you draw the parallels mostly yourself. If you are looking to be spoon fed ideas this may not be for you. He really just gives it all to you and lets you at it yourself. I also don't know if I was satisfied with the wrap up section and his ending of the work, BUT once again for the absolute thrill of enjoying new and interesting information he gets five stars from me. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3827861902038864936" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_223500220" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/223500220" />
    <a title="Rebecca" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5769212-rebecca"><img alt="Rebecca" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1486312228p2/5769212.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/223500220?book_show_action=true">Oct 14, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rebecca" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rebecca" href="/user/show/5769212-rebecca">Rebecca</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer223500220" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6821186213616452954">I read this, like many people, because I know how influential it was. I studied English in college, and this book always kept cropping up. So I thought to myself, maybe if I read this, I'll have a greater understanding of Modernist writers. <br/><br/>Right...<br/><br/>How to describe this? 850 pages of poorly argued drivel. The only part worth reading is the section on sympathetic magic. That part at least actually seems to be going somewhere and actually makes sense. It's an interesting and intelligent way of thin</span>
  <span id="freeText6821186213616452954" style="display:none">I read this, like many people, because I know how influential it was. I studied English in college, and this book always kept cropping up. So I thought to myself, maybe if I read this, I'll have a greater understanding of Modernist writers. <br /><br />Right...<br /><br />How to describe this? 850 pages of poorly argued drivel. The only part worth reading is the section on sympathetic magic. That part at least actually seems to be going somewhere and actually makes sense. It's an interesting and intelligent way of thinking about rituals throughout the world and throughout the centuries. <br /><br />This "study", however, soon dissolves into an endless list of examples that are both mind-numbing and unnecessary. Any semblance of an argument or point soon becomes lost in the pages and pages of almost useless descriptions of every single ritual Frazer has ever read about. I can't believe that there is a 12 volume version of this floating around somewhere. I would rather gouge my eyes out with spoons.<br /><br />There are two main problems with the Golden Bough:<br />1. Frazer is a terrible writer who can't string together a coherent argument and utterly FAILS at trimming the fat,and...<br />2. Frazer is full of shit. He tries to relate things that have absolutely no relationship merely based on "assuming" that there is an "obvious" similarity between two completely unrelated rituals. Every time I read the words "It is safe to assume that..." or "obviously/clearly" I wanted to punch this guy in the face. It is safe to assume that he obviously is grasping at straws to formulate a thesis that is clearly full of holes. <br /><br />About a couple hundred pages in, I started skipping entire [page long] paragraphs of examples and skimming through the parts that were either extra ridiculous or extra (extra!) boring. I'm convinced that this is the only way to get through this thing. <br /><br />So what have I learned by reading The Golden Bough? I have learned that not only were the modernist writers twisted masochists for reading this but that so am I. <br /><br />Let's leave The Golden Bough in 1922. Where it belongs. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6821186213616452954" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_610262085" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/610262085" />
    <a title="Walter Five" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19826826-walter-five"><img alt="Walter Five" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1367103451p2/19826826.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/610262085?book_show_action=true">May 08, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Walter Five" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Walter Five" href="/user/show/19826826-walter-five">Walter Five</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/6649474-the-golden-bough">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19826826-walter-five?shelf=archeology">archeology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19826826-walter-five?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19826826-walter-five?shelf=sociology">sociology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19826826-walter-five?shelf=religion">religion</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer610262085" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer127770489400917256">This is not a "review" of the classic "Golden Bough." This is a review of several hardback and soft back editions available to the collector which are complimentary to each other's content and pagination. The purchaser may wish to take care in selecting which edition of the Golden Bough they consider for purchase. Several hardbound editions exist.<br/><br/>The most common edition is the 2-volume abridged 4th edition,this is the edition supervised by Graves widow; it is woefully incomplete. It seems Mrs. G</span>
  <span id="freeText127770489400917256" style="display:none">This is not a "review" of the classic "Golden Bough." This is a review of several hardback and soft back editions available to the collector which are complimentary to each other's content and pagination. The purchaser may wish to take care in selecting which edition of the Golden Bough they consider for purchase. Several hardbound editions exist.<br /><br />The most common edition is the 2-volume abridged 4th edition,this is the edition supervised by Graves widow; it is woefully incomplete. It seems Mrs. Graves was bothered by the Judeo-Christian references as myth, and reedited the 13 volume set to the bone.<br /><br />But still, it is to the 3rd Edition that most collectors will want to turn. The 3rd edition has twelve volumes, and a 1-volume afterward, which, when assembled look rather like a set of encyclopedias. There have been twelve reliable and inter-changable printings from 1911 to 1990 which may allow the collector the opportunity to assemble a complete set of mis-matching volumes, as I have done, spending less than $240 to assemble the entire series, or averaging less than $20.00 a volume. The original publishers to look for are MacMillan &amp; Co. and St. Martin's Press. One cannot vouch for the print accuracy of the plethora of "On Demand" editions, I avoid them after a couple bad experiences with other titles--too many transcription errors.<br /><br />The numbering on these volumes is sometimes confusing-- The on-line seller's lists at ALibris and ABE books always get them wrong, so you may want to do a general search for "Frazer" "Golden Bough Part" and compare the listing to the contents below (as printed on the dust jacket back of the 1990 edition):<br />The 13 books are divided into 9 parts:<br />Part I Vols. 1 &amp; 2, The Magic Art and the Evolution of Kings<br />Part II Taboo and The Perils of the Soul<br />Part III The Dying God<br />Part IV Adonis, Attis Osiris Vols. 1 &amp; 2<br />Part V Spirits of the Corn and of the Wild Vols. 1&amp;2<br />Part VI The Scapegoat<br />Part VII Balder the Beautiful: The Fire Festivals of Europe and<br />The Doctrine of the External Soul<br />Part VIII Bibliography and General Index<br />Part IX Aftermath: A Supplement to "The Golden Bough."<br /><br />With a little preserverance you can assemble a set within a few months. These books ought to be required reading to any student of religion, history, sociology, and occult sciences. </span>
  <a data-text-id="127770489400917256" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_427265102" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/427265102" />
    <a title="Laural" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11348002-laural"><img alt="Laural" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1349329030p2/11348002.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/427265102?book_show_action=true">Oct 03, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laural" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laural" href="/user/show/11348002-laural">Laural</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer427265102" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13536835476701924291">The Golden Bough is no doubt an exercise in patience. To be clear, I have not finished this book, and will not for many years. This book takes time to digest and fully understand, but once that time is taken to contemplate it, literally everything that can be seen in the world opens up to the insights that are provided. Expecting to read this book once, without careful pause and effort, is akin to attempting to understand the enlightenment of the ages in an afternoon. I can see how many parts of</span>
  <span id="freeText13536835476701924291" style="display:none">The Golden Bough is no doubt an exercise in patience. To be clear, I have not finished this book, and will not for many years. This book takes time to digest and fully understand, but once that time is taken to contemplate it, literally everything that can be seen in the world opens up to the insights that are provided. Expecting to read this book once, without careful pause and effort, is akin to attempting to understand the enlightenment of the ages in an afternoon. I can see how many parts of The Golden Bough may come off as elitist or racist, but really Frazer was simply expressing his personal preference for the ideas that shaped his own perspective, and I think he is clear in recognizing this. Considering that most people approaching spirituality are unable to see beyond their own loads of worldview even enough to attempt to understand another, The Golden Bough is really a shining example of tolerance and understanding. Again, I have not read the entire work, and there may be unforgivable racisms that I have yet to read. Even so, the positive impact of The Golden Bough on the world is undeniable.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13536835476701924291" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer6631753961816081353">Book Description<br/>A classic study of the beliefs and institutions of mankind, and the progress through magic and religion to scientific thought, The Golden Bough has a unique status in modern anthropology and literature. First published in 1890, The Golden Bough was eventually issued in a twelve-volume edition (1906-15) which was abridged in 1922 by the author and his wife.   <span id="freeText6631753961816081353" style="display:none">Book Description<br />A classic study of the beliefs and institutions of mankind, and the progress through magic and religion to scientific thought, The Golden Bough has a unique status in modern anthropology and literature. First published in 1890, The Golden Bough was eventually issued in a twelve-volume edition (1906-15) which was abridged in 1922 by the author and his wife. That abridgement has never been reconsidered for a modern audience. In it some of the more controversial passages were dropped, including Frazer's daring speculations on the Crucifixion of Christ. For the first time this one-volume edition restores Frazer's bolder theories and sets them within the framework of a valuable introduction and notes. A seminal work of modern anthropolgy, The Golden Bough also influenced many twentieth-century writers, including D H Lawrence, T S Eliot, and Wyndham Lewis. Its discussion of magical types, the sacrificial killing of kings, the dying god, and the scapegoat is given fresh pertinence in this new edition. <br /><br />About the Author<br />Sir J. G. Frazer (1854-1941) was fellow of Trinity, Cambridge, and appointed to the first named Chair of Social Anthropology in Liverpool. Robert Frazer is Directer of Studies in English at Trinity College, Cambridge. He is the author of The Making of `The Golden Bough' and Sir James Frazer and the Literary Imagination, both for MacMillan in 1990) </span>
  <a data-text-id="6631753961816081353" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer39117517" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11850944864360578506">As <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/9810.Albert_Einstein' title='Albert Einstein' rel='nofollow noopener'>Einstein</a> is to physics, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/12793.Charles_Darwin' title='Charles Darwin' rel='nofollow noopener'>Darwin</a> to biology, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7084.Karl_Marx' title='Karl Marx' rel='nofollow noopener'>Marx</a> to social theory and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/10017.Sigmund_Freud' title='Sigmund Freud' rel='nofollow noopener'>Freud</a> to psychology, so is Sir James G. Frazer to anthropology. <i>The Golden Bough</i> is an ambitious work in which Frazer works with field reports describing superstitions and practices, and theorizes that the folk rituals he discusses can be traced back to ancient times and an annual event in the forest at Nemi. From a contemporary point of view, it can be argued that Frazer’s approach is reductive, and indeed anthropology has </span>
  <span id="freeText11850944864360578506" style="display:none">As <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/9810.Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein" rel="nofollow noopener">Einstein</a> is to physics, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/12793.Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin" rel="nofollow noopener">Darwin</a> to biology, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7084.Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx" rel="nofollow noopener">Marx</a> to social theory and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/10017.Sigmund_Freud" title="Sigmund Freud" rel="nofollow noopener">Freud</a> to psychology, so is Sir James G. Frazer to anthropology. <i>The Golden Bough</i> is an ambitious work in which Frazer works with field reports describing superstitions and practices, and theorizes that the folk rituals he discusses can be traced back to ancient times and an annual event in the forest at Nemi. From a contemporary point of view, it can be argued that Frazer’s approach is reductive, and indeed anthropology has largely discredited his work (even as psychological theory has moved beyond Freudian thought). Nevertheless, Frazer’s work supplies the reader with an interesting perspective on such rituals as May Day, Halloween, Thanksgiving, Christmas and New Year’s Day.</span>

Eliot and even Apocalypse Now. <br /><br />It's a bit dated, particularly in its sticking to the "primitive savage" evolves into "sophisticated civilization" model, but alot of the basic principals are still very sound. <br /><br />Frazer starts a single incident, a Latin ritual of a King of the Forest, who is ritually killed and replaced by his successor. <br /><br />He uses this a launching pad for a far reaching, global discussion of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7815.The_Year_of_Magical_Thinking" title="The Year of Magical Thinking by Joan Didion" rel="nofollow noopener">magical thinking</a>, divine kings, birth/rebirth cycles, and the psychology of killing the god.<br /><br />I recommend it. <div id="review_78934342" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/78934342" />
    <a title="F.G. Cottam" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1746984-f-g-cottam"><img alt="F.G. Cottam" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442479259p2/1746984.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/78934342?book_show_action=true">Nov 25, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="F.G. Cottam" class="user" itemprop="url" name="F.G. Cottam" href="/user/show/1746984-f-g-cottam">F.G. Cottam</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780684818504" href="/book/show/165076.The_Illustrated_Golden_Bough">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer78934342" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13225852220032581207">This is a brilliant and disturbing distillation of Frazer's much longer and doggedly scholarly original work. I've come across it late (the edition I've got is dated 1978). I bought it second hand four years ago at Ventnor Rare Books on the Isle of Wight and have only just got around to reading it. If anything, the illustrations make the text all the more shocking. Reading about belief systems where human sacrifice was commonplace is one thing - seeing those sacrifices depicted in contemporary a</span>
  <span id="freeText13225852220032581207" style="display:none">This is a brilliant and disturbing distillation of Frazer's much longer and doggedly scholarly original work. I've come across it late (the edition I've got is dated 1978). I bought it second hand four years ago at Ventnor Rare Books on the Isle of Wight and have only just got around to reading it. If anything, the illustrations make the text all the more shocking. Reading about belief systems where human sacrifice was commonplace is one thing - seeing those sacrifices depicted in contemporary artwork is quite another. I came away from this book reminded of just how imaginative and cruel human beings can be. And I've been convinced anew that our appetite for myth and superstition is pretty much insatiable. Every chapter is comprehensive and fascinating. I would recommend this impressive book to anyone with an interest in anthropology,<br />history, archeology, religion, magic, folklore or classical mythology. A lot of books are described as unputdownable. This one actually earns the accolade.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13225852220032581207" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_722838179" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/722838179" />
    <a title="Bob Nichols" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2105523-bob-nichols"><img alt="Bob Nichols" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1314751863p2/2105523.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/722838179?book_show_action=true">Sep 18, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bob Nichols" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bob Nichols" href="/user/show/2105523-bob-nichols">Bob Nichols</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780875990361" href="/book/show/2588902-the-new-golden-bough">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer722838179" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11797451450719936894">This is an abridgment of a much larger work by Frazer that compiles, categorizes and interprets the belief systems of very old cultures. It's easy to get overwhelmed by the extensive listing of examples that Frazer provides unless these are viewed as attempts by these cultures to understand and control nature through magical practices. These practices for Frazer appear to manifest deeper structures surrounding human need and fear. In short, Frazer writes, they reflect "the essential similiaritie</span>
  <span id="freeText11797451450719936894" style="display:none">This is an abridgment of a much larger work by Frazer that compiles, categorizes and interprets the belief systems of very old cultures. It's easy to get overwhelmed by the extensive listing of examples that Frazer provides unless these are viewed as attempts by these cultures to understand and control nature through magical practices. These practices for Frazer appear to manifest deeper structures surrounding human need and fear. In short, Frazer writes, they reflect "the essential similiarities of man's chief wants everywhere and at all times." <br /><br />On this issue of causality, Frazer believes there's a progression from magic to religion to science. As the editor and abridger of Frazer, Gaster is critical of that point of view, stating that it "has now been shown to be a mere product of late nineteenth-century evolutionism, without adequate basis." But how Frazer's perspective is without merit is not clear. In his commentary, Gaster's general criticisms seem obtuse or nitpicky and, at times, he seems like he is trying to preserve a special place for religion, as distinct from primitive magic. Given this, it's hard not to wonder what of Judaism and Christianity Gaster left out of this abridgment. For Frazer's part, science is much in use these days relative to earlier times and that is likely his point, even though a good part of humankind still believes in forces from an unseen world that are propitiated by prayer and such. Gaster is also critical of Frazer's sources of information, suggesting that the Golden Bough rests on a somewhat flimsy ground, even though Frazer extensively documents his studies, drawing from well-known and well-cited sources (e.g., Pliny, Boas, Plutarch, Herodotus, Schoolcraft, Hume, Ovid, Livy, Plato). And, in contrast to Gaster's various authoritative assertions about Frazer's opinions, Frazer offers his specific interpretations with some humility, suggesting an openess to alternative explanations. "All our theories" of primitive man, Frazer states, "fall far short of certainty; the utmost we can aspire to in such matters is a reasonable degree of probability." <br /><br />This (700 page, plus) abridgment is rich in detail on the practices of earlier humans and Frazer's interpretation of their meaning seems reasonable enough. In my view, they are more understandable and perhaps more probable than, say, Campbell's explanations. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11797451450719936894" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_571315592" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/571315592" />
    <a title="Bradley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley"><img alt="Bradley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1508811508p2/4213258.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/571315592?book_show_action=true">Mar 26, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bradley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bradley" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley">Bradley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=metaphysics">metaphysics</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer571315592" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5342087529183231413">So far, while it does a lot of mythological name-dropping, and the very thin veil of a theme seems accurate, I'm tempted to say that this book is a real mess. Goddesses with mixed up attributes, bald-faced assumptions about ancient societies, and rampant misspellings almost turn me off. And yet, I have stamina. I have fortitude. I shall endure another escaped slave trying to murder me so he can break off the branch of my sacred tree and so take my place.<br/><br/>Some random, albeit unfortunate, quotes:<br/><br/>"A</span>
  <span id="freeText5342087529183231413" style="display:none">So far, while it does a lot of mythological name-dropping, and the very thin veil of a theme seems accurate, I'm tempted to say that this book is a real mess. Goddesses with mixed up attributes, bald-faced assumptions about ancient societies, and rampant misspellings almost turn me off. And yet, I have stamina. I have fortitude. I shall endure another escaped slave trying to murder me so he can break off the branch of my sacred tree and so take my place.<br /><br />Some random, albeit unfortunate, quotes:<br /><br />"And they were forced to lay upon some erections."<br /><br />"And she was given the gift of a cock."<br /><br /><br />Seriously enough, I've been very impressed by the work. Ok, so on my ebook reader, it only runs up to a little under 1500 pages, and there are at least a dozen accounts as proof of each point. I cannot, in good faith, find fault with much of his conclusions. I was astonished to realize how many assumptions I had held about Osiris were completely balderdash. At least I've been put to rights about the real reason he was worshiped. Hint: it wasn't because they never found his penis.<br /><br />Overall, the main themes are drilled into our skulls so thoroughly that there's no way we could ever forget them, even if we tried. The best and the worst that I can say for this work is that it is very thorough. I can honestly say I've heard discussions of the many themes, as I'm sure most of us have; fertility deities, all manifestations thereof. <br /><br />What I was most astonished to feel, after reading this work, was a great sadness. I look back at all of the thousands of cultures that have independently worshiped the same principles over time and see how they were systematically wiped out as "poppycock", and I wonder about the now-lost depth of understanding that is now lost to time and chronos... and I wonder if Uranus ever did find his penis.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5342087529183231413" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        rated it

  <span id="freeText13669398343767481508" style="display:none">As with so many of my scholarly books on mythology and comparative studies of myths and legends, I read them in an ongoing sort of way. <span id="freeTextContainer12694959274208576849">It's important to bear in mind that this book is almost 100 years old, and therefore some of the author's attitudes are . . . narrow-minded, to say the least. However, Frazer is more open than usual for his time, I think, and his look at folkloric and religious customs is exhaustive. (I read the abridged version, which was over 800 pages long and meandered widely through numerous cultures, so I can only imagine what the unabridged <i>Golden Bough</i> is like.)   <span id="freeText12694959274208576849" style="display:none">It's important to bear in mind that this book is almost 100 years old, and therefore some of the author's attitudes are . . . narrow-minded, to say the least. However, Frazer is more open than usual for his time, I think, and his look at folkloric and religious customs is exhaustive. (I read the abridged version, which was over 800 pages long and meandered widely through numerous cultures, so I can only imagine what the unabridged <i>Golden Bough</i> is like.) He ties a great many disparate ideas together, and for a poet and fiction writer who likes mythological themes, there's an embarrassment of richest to be had.</span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer500581928" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10682114022944748274">I read this years ago when I was in my twenties and I remember this book making quite an impression. I am even more impressed with the ideas covered in it. The religious beliefs about sacrifice, ritual and dying and rising gods, and vegetation symbolism for this death and rebirth and ritualistic eating of the god and the cycle of seasons and stars. I hope I am hinting at a common pattern in mythology that would later be picked up by maybe at least one of the world's major religions. This book is</span>
  <span id="freeText10682114022944748274" style="display:none">I read this years ago when I was in my twenties and I remember this book making quite an impression. I am even more impressed with the ideas covered in it. The religious beliefs about sacrifice, ritual and dying and rising gods, and vegetation symbolism for this death and rebirth and ritualistic eating of the god and the cycle of seasons and stars. I hope I am hinting at a common pattern in mythology that would later be picked up by maybe at least one of the world's major religions. This book is rich looking at universal cultural elements in magic and religion that keep popping up from long ago to more modern incarnations. Long book but worth the time.</span>

  <span id="freeText12081529429248943120" style="display:none">It was a great favour done to mankind when Frazer and his wife chose to condense the original twelve volumes into one volume; even the single volume appears so repetitive one can only imagine the ordeal that s/he that tries to read all the twelve has to undergo.<br /><br />That said, I believe that Frazer's work (twelve volumes or one) is an immense contribution to the realm of anthropology - though one may not agree with all its contentions, it undoubtedly provides one structured framework for the entirety of myth, lore and legend of mankind to be compared with and contrasted against, and for conclusions as agree with individual students drawn. <br /><br />(Oops, pretty long sentence ... I hope the grammar is correct)<br /><br />Speaking for myself, the book is an immense help as a structure for my own studies on Indo-Germanic mythology, religion and cultural practices.</span>

    <a title="Andrew" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/175164-andrew"><img alt="Andrew" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1183811586p2/175164.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/86527837?book_show_action=true">Jan 24, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andrew" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andrew" href="/user/show/175164-andrew">Andrew</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title=" 9788130700212" href="/book/show/1395351.The_Golden_Bough">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/175164-andrew?shelf=anthropology">anthropology</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer86527837" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2825255695860984798">This book is swarming with folklore, mythology and animistic ritual examples. Pretty much the entire book is dedicated to short explanations of ritual practices from all over the globe. What it lacks is much in the way of a linear, coherent argument or point.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1369728581" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1369728581" />
    <a title="Individualfrog" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6433504-individualfrog"><img alt="Individualfrog" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1316894675p2/6433504.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1369728581?book_show_action=true">Aug 21, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Individualfrog" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Individualfrog" href="/user/show/6433504-individualfrog">Individualfrog</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/25170507-the-golden-bough-abridged">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6433504-individualfrog?shelf=enormous-enveloping-books">enormous-enveloping-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6433504-individualfrog?shelf=nonfiction-for-readers">nonfiction-for-readers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6433504-individualfrog?shelf=secret-research">secret-research</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6433504-individualfrog?shelf=medieval-christian">medieval-christian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6433504-individualfrog?shelf=pagan-roots">pagan-roots</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6433504-individualfrog?shelf=ideas-weird-and-wonderful">ideas-weird-and-wonderful</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1369728581" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12116874441324332038">This is a book, like one of Borges's favorite conceits, with a secret meaning underneath its stated one. Explicitly, Frazer's aim is to "explain" the mysterious tradition of the priest of the sacred grove of Diana at the lake of Nemi, who gained his post by stealing a "golden bough" from the grove and killing the current priest, taking his place, until killed in his turn by the next contender. Frazer recounts and then dismisses the explanations in the classical sources, saying they are clearly a</span>
  <span id="freeText12116874441324332038" style="display:none">This is a book, like one of Borges's favorite conceits, with a secret meaning underneath its stated one. Explicitly, Frazer's aim is to "explain" the mysterious tradition of the priest of the sacred grove of Diana at the lake of Nemi, who gained his post by stealing a "golden bough" from the grove and killing the current priest, taking his place, until killed in his turn by the next contender. Frazer recounts and then dismisses the explanations in the classical sources, saying they are clearly ahistorical late attempts to rationalize a rite so ancient and archaic that its practitioners had long forgotten the true meaning of what they were doing. So, by a process somewhat like historical linguistics (at least to this interested layman) he uses a process of inference and analogy to trace backwards and reconstruct a speculative, unattested religion of great antiquity (of what Frazer calls the Aryans and we today would call the Proto-Indo-Europeans), of which the Nemi succession is a singular late survival, a sort of coelacanth of culture. Using the evidence of comparative folklore and folk superstition gathered from all over the world, he seeks to explain why the priest of Diana was killed, why the golden bough was plucked, even why the later myths accrued to it. Or so he claims.<br /><br />But the true meaning of the book, as Frazer is at no real lengths to conceal, is to undermine Christianity and any truth claims of the New Testament, including the existence of a historical Jesus (along with whatever digs he can make towards the Old) by revealing great similarities to many points of doctrine, practice, and story in scripture to a vast and bewildering array of religious practices the world over. In exactly the same way as he argues for the true origins of the priest of Nemi's death and replacement, he give the reader evidence (<i>without</i> explicitly arguing, leading the reader to make the inference for themself) for the real origins and meanings of Christianity. From obvious pagan borrowings like Easter and Christmas, to the fundamental concept of a God made incarnate and sacrificed to Himself as atonement for others' sins, to the central rite of the Eucharist, piece by piece all the specifics of Christianity are made to look not so specific, not so unique, but rather to be borrowed bits of far more ancient traditions, just another entry in the "melancholy record of human error and folly which has engaged our attention in this book."<br /><br />Now I had heard of this beforehand, and it was very interesting to me, reminding me of Borges's idea of Donne's <i>Biathanatos</i> as secretly arguing "the idea of a God who creates the universe in order to create His own gallows." I'm not sure how I would have felt about it otherwise. Would I have felt cheated? As it was I was a bit disappointed by how <i>not</i> secret this secret is. The anti-Christian argument is barely concealed, especially towards the end, and while it's certainly less boring than our contemporary idiot atheist blowhards, Frazer can be quite as condescending and obnoxious as they can. When he first ventures into the notion of the scapegoat, his meaning is perfectly clear:<br /><blockquote>The accumulated misfortunes and sins of the whole people are sometimes laid upon the dying god, who is supposed to bear them away forever, leaving the people innocent and happy. The notion that we can transfer our guilt and sufferings to some other being who will bear them for us is familiar to the savage mind. It arises from a very obvious confusion between the physical and the mental, between the material and the immaterial. Because it is possible to shift a load of wood, stones, or what not, from our own back to the back of another, the savage fancies that it is equally possible to shift the burden of his pains and sorrows to another, who will suffer them in his stead. Upon this idea he acts, and the result is an endless number of very unamiable devices for palming off upon someone else the trouble which a man shrinks from bearing himself. In short, the principle of vicarious suffering is commonly understood and practised by races who stand on a low level of social and intellectual culture. In the following pages I shall illustrate the theory and the practice as they are found among savages in all their naked simplicity, undisguised by the refinements of metaphysics and the subtleties of theology.</blockquote><br /><br />To me this is overdoing it. We got the point long before, and the final line amounts to poking me in the ribs saying "get it? get it?" Yes, James, I got it. Another point to be made about this is that, though it takes an enormous amount of space, the scapegoat idea has no apparent connection whatever to the cult of Diana at Nemi. (Speaking of space, I read Frazer's own abridgment, and though I am not the abridging type--I just recently read the entirety of Gibbon's <i>Decline and Fall</i>--I think I would recommend it to anyone. The argument must be exactly the same, only fortified with more examples, but the examples, even when interesting, which is not particularly often, must have been superseded long since by better, more accurate and less racist research, so there is no point to subjecting yourself to them all.) Generally speaking the explicit argument about the meaning of the Nemi priesthood is much weaker than the implicit one about Christianity. It takes only a mention to relate eating the god to the Eucharist, or the sacrifice of a portion of bear meat to the bear-god itself to Christ's sacrifice on the cross, the analogy is simple and natural; but it takes a very precarious bridge of completely speculative and unlikely conjectures to relate these things to the priest of Diana, who was not a god, was not sacrificed per se, who did not even by inference take on anyone's sins, etc. We must accept Frazer's various guesses and analogies--that the priest was a priest-king, that he represented a god, that this god is a god of the wood, that this wood god is like other vegetable gods, that various dying-resurrecting gods are also vegetable gods, that these symbolic deaths and resurrections were always acted out literally in human sacrifices, that human sacrifices are something like scapegoats, etc. It is fascinating to watch Frazer construct these tottering structures of thought, but not very convincing, even when you are willing to disregard the many painful reminders of his Edwardian intellectual millieu: the casual racism, the use of dubious field research, the vulgar teleologism, all the things which are hopefully obvious and now unacceptable in contemporary anthropology.<br /><br />The parallels between Christian dogma and a vast and bewildering array of other traditions from other cultures is certainly striking and interesting (and, as <a href="http://www.vqronline.org/essay/golden-bough" rel="nofollow noopener">John Peale Bishop wrote</a>, in the aftermath of World War I, not even necessarily negative: "For it is also possible for us, regarding Christianity in the light cast from the sacred tree at Nemi, to find that it has gained as much as it has lost. Since it had already forfeited in our minds any special claims it may once have had as a supernatural revelation, these should be counted an inconsiderable loss. By extending its existence into the dark backward and abyss of time, it has gained, not only the respectability of age, but another authenticity.") But unfortunately, for me, the Nemi priesthood and classical paganism in general is actually much more interesting, and so it is rather disappointing to see it getting short shrift this way. I'm not a Christian, and these sorts of Scooby Doo unmaskings of Christianity can never have the illicit taboo thrill that they have for those who believe or are oppressed by not believing. I'm closer to a pagan, and I find actual ancient paganism endlessly interesting. (It's a conundrum to me why, when all the examples are written in the same flat slightly sarcastic way, I should be so much more interested in some than in others; the Greeks and Romans and Syrians and Egyptians are always interesting, whereas the customs of Australian Aborigines and Germanic/Norse pre-Christian myths and French peasants are frequently extremely tedious. "Racism," joked my brother, but I'm not totally convinced he's not right, on some level somewhere. Some prejudice obtains in me against my own northern European roots, as well as the tediously elaborate magic spells described by imperialist colonial invaders of Africa.) No doubt some newer text can be found on ancient Latin religion which would have the sort of stuff I missed in this book. But modern scholars are usually disappointing; generally speaking, they can't write (whereas Frazer, when not recounting endless examples of the same European custom from the same European region--sometimes he's going town to town in Bohemia or Germany and recounting every tiny meaningless variation--can write beautifully) and their ideas are tentative and boringly expressed, with all the crazy daring of Frazer squeezed out of them.<br /><br />But overall I certainly feel I understood how this book became, as it says on the back of my copy, A WORLD CLASSIC; though I am not super-convinced of its accuracy, as I say it is sort of thrilling to follow Frazer's chain of A--&gt;B?--&gt;C??--D???? and to thereby imagine a sort of primordial ur-religion. Like geology or evolutionary biology or Jung or historical linguistics or Biblical "higher criticism", to deduce the depths from the surface, the past from the accidental traces of the present. I think it's worth reading still, though not anymore as a "tumultuous encyclopedia" (quoting the back again), because I will not believe a single thing written about other cultures' practices without corroboration, instead as an example of an audacious mind at work at a double project, his real object hidden in plain sight.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12116874441324332038" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer1001948750786758570">Less about magic and religion and more a catalog of superstitions throughout history (although, to be fair, the three things generally get melded together by people who believe in these kinds of things). <br/><br/>This book is dense and Frazer is doing a lot of guesswork throughout, but I was never bored and Fraser's obvious enthusiasm for the topic is infectious.</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('GGVbDsBGnIpgTpjwZvu4RtsvJEkFyZ6y7Ff/ZI7rYI75c5YXZ5KM3YLPAIgyliIjVHicGYeNQq2YNVQ8vW1RGw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('bM3mWy1rmoQyO3on49uBTy0/iRxrM2380ziTrZzZ6iqN2ytCir+K09C64l+3thsqomgxTOl3seOnWjj1r1/bvw==')})
  }

      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_28022'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/28022.Shamanism?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Shamanism: Archaic Techniques of Ecstasy<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/14521.Mircea_Eliade?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Mircea Eliade<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.22 avg rating &mdash; 2,274 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1951\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer7605085288175980756\">First published in 1951, \"Shamanism\" soon became the standard work in the study of this mysterious & fascinating phenomenon. Writing as the founder of the modern study of the history of religion, Romanian emigre--scholar Mircea Eliade (1907-86) surve<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText7605085288175980756\" style=\"display:none\">First published in 1951, &quot;Shamanism&quot; soon became the standard work in the study of this mysterious &amp; fascinating phenomenon. Writing as the founder of the modern study of the history of religion, Romanian emigre--scholar Mircea Eliade (1907-86) surveys the practice of Shamanism over two &amp; a half millennia of human history, moving from the Shamanic traditions of Siberia &amp; Central Asia--where Shamanism was first observed--to North &amp; South America, Indonesia, Tibet, China &amp; beyond. In this authoritative survey, Eliade illuminates the magico-religious life of societies that give primacy of place to the figure of the Shaman--at once magician &amp; medicine man, healer &amp; miracle-doer, priest, mystic &amp; poet. Synthesizing the approaches of psychology, sociology &amp; ethnology, &quot;Shamanism&quot; will remain for years to come the reference book of choice for those intrigued by this practice.<\/span>\n Reading The Varieties of Religious Experience, it is easy to see why. Applying his analytic clarity to religious accounts from a variety of sources, James elaborates a pluralistic framework in which &quot;the divine can mean no single quality, it must mean a group of qualities, by being champions of which in alternation, different men may all find worthy missions.&quot; It\'s an intellectual call for serious religious tolerance--indeed, respect--the vitality of
